<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Niobium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 41</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Niobium,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">41</span></sub>Nb</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="image" title="A lump of gray shining crystals with hexagonal facetting"><img alt="A lump of gray shining crystals with hexagonal facetting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/220px-Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" width="220" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/330px-Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/440px-Niobium_crystals_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5356" data-file-height="3229" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ffc0c0">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ny-<span style="font-size:90%">OH</span>-bee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
gray metallic, bluish when oxidized</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;">&#x20;(<i>A</i><sub>r,&#160;standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_niobium" title="Template:Infobox niobium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_niobium" title="Template talk:Infobox niobium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_niobium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Niobium</b>, formerly known as <b>columbium</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Nb</b> (formerly Cb) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 41. It is a soft, grey, crystalline, <a href="/wiki/Ductile" class="mw-redirect" title="Ductile">ductile</a> <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a>, often found in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Pyrochlore" title="Pyrochlore">pyrochlore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Columbite" title="Columbite">columbite</a>, hence the former name "columbium". Its name comes from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Niobe" title="Niobe">Niobe</a>, who was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Tantalus" title="Tantalus">Tantalus</a>, the namesake of <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a>. The name reflects the great similarity between the two elements in their physical and chemical properties, making them difficult to distinguish.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The English chemist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hatchett" title="Charles Hatchett">Charles Hatchett</a> reported a new element similar to tantalum in 1801 and named it columbium. In 1809, the English chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Hyde_Wollaston" title="William Hyde Wollaston">William Hyde Wollaston</a> wrongly concluded that tantalum and columbium were identical. The German chemist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rose" title="Heinrich Rose">Heinrich Rose</a> determined in 1846 that tantalum ores contain a second element, which he named niobium. In 1864 and 1865, a series of scientific findings clarified that niobium and columbium were the same element (as distinguished from tantalum), and for a century both names were used interchangeably. Niobium was officially adopted as the name of the element in 1949, but the name columbium remains in current use in metallurgy in the United States.
</p><p>It was not until the early 20th century that niobium was first used commercially. Brazil is the leading producer of niobium and <a href="/wiki/Ferroniobium" title="Ferroniobium">ferroniobium</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> of 60–70% niobium with iron. Niobium is used mostly in alloys, the largest part in special <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> such as that used in gas <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>. Although these alloys contain a maximum of 0.1%, the small percentage of niobium enhances the strength of the steel. The temperature stability of niobium-containing <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">superalloys</a> is important for its use in <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket engines</a>.
</p><p>Niobium is used in various <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconducting</a> materials. These <a href="/wiki/Type-II_superconductor" title="Type-II superconductor">superconducting alloys</a>, also containing <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, are widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">superconducting magnets</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI scanners</a>. Other applications of niobium include welding, nuclear industries, electronics, optics, <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">numismatics</a>, and jewelry. In the last two applications, the low toxicity and iridescence produced by <a href="/wiki/Anodizing" title="Anodizing">anodization</a> are highly desired properties.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:107px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Hatchett.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Oval black and white painting of a man with a prominent shirt collar and necktie" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Charles_Hatchett.jpg" width="105" height="135" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="105" data-file-height="135" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Hatchett.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Hatchett" title="Charles Hatchett">Charles Hatchett</a> identified the element <i>columbium</i> within a mineral discovered in Connecticut, US.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sommer,_Giorgio_(1834-1914)_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Black and white image of a marmor sculpture of a bowing woman with a child nestling in her lap" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg/110px-Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg" width="110" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg/165px-Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg/220px-Sommer%2C_Giorgio_%281834-1914%29_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg 2x" data-file-width="362" data-file-height="567" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sommer,_Giorgio_(1834-1914)_-_n._2990_-_Niobe_madre_-_Firenze.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Picture of a Hellenistic sculpture representing <i>Niobe</i> by <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Sommer" title="Giorgio Sommer">Giorgio Sommer</a></div></div></div>
<p>Niobium was <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">identified</a> by English chemist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hatchett" title="Charles Hatchett">Charles Hatchett</a> in 1801.<sup id="cite_ref-Hatchett_1802a_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatchett_1802a-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hatchett_1802b_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatchett_1802b-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hatchett_1802c_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatchett_1802c-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> He found a new element in a mineral sample that had been sent to England from <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, United States in 1734 by John Winthrop F.R.S. (grandson of <a href="/wiki/John_Winthrop_the_Younger" title="John Winthrop the Younger">John Winthrop the Younger</a>) and named the mineral <i>columbite</i> and the new element <i>columbium</i> after <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a></i>, the poetical name for the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Noyes_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noyes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1853_Mining_Journal_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1853_Mining_Journal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The <i>columbium</i> discovered by Hatchett was probably a mixture of the new element with tantalum.<sup id="cite_ref-Noyes_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noyes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Subsequently, there was considerable confusion<sup id="cite_ref-Wolla_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolla-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> over the difference between columbium (niobium) and the closely related tantalum. In 1809, English chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Hyde_Wollaston" title="William Hyde Wollaston">William Hyde Wollaston</a> compared the oxides derived from both columbium—columbite, with a density 5.918&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, and tantalum—<a href="/wiki/Tantalite" title="Tantalite">tantalite</a>, with a density over 8&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, and concluded that the two oxides, despite the significant difference in density, were identical; thus he kept the name tantalum.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolla_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolla-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This conclusion was disputed in 1846 by German chemist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rose" title="Heinrich Rose">Heinrich Rose</a>, who argued that there were two different elements in the tantalite sample, and named them after children of <a href="/wiki/Tantalus" title="Tantalus">Tantalus</a>: <i>niobium</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Niobe" title="Niobe">Niobe</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Pelopium" title="Pelopium">pelopium</a></i> (from <a href="/wiki/Pelops" title="Pelops">Pelops</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Pelop_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pelop-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This confusion arose from the minimal observed differences between tantalum and niobium. The claimed new elements <i>pelopium</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ilmenium" title="Ilmenium">ilmenium</a></i>, and <i>dianium</i><sup id="cite_ref-Dianium_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dianium-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> were in fact identical to niobium or mixtures of niobium and tantalum.<sup id="cite_ref-Ilmen_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilmen-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The differences between tantalum and niobium were unequivocally demonstrated in 1864 by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wilhelm_Blomstrand" title="Christian Wilhelm Blomstrand">Christian Wilhelm Blomstrand</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ilmen_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilmen-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Louis_J._Troost" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis J. Troost">Louis J. Troost</a>, who determined the formulas of some of the compounds in 1865<sup id="cite_ref-Ilmen_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilmen-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gupta_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and finally by Swiss chemist <a href="/wiki/Jean_Charles_Galissard_de_Marignac" title="Jean Charles Galissard de Marignac">Jean Charles Galissard de Marignac</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> in 1866, who all proved that there were only two elements. Articles on <i>ilmenium</i> continued to appear until 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>De Marignac was the first to prepare the metal in 1864, when he <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduced</a> niobium chloride by heating it in an atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nauti_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nauti-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Although de Marignac was able to produce tantalum-free niobium on a larger scale by 1866, it was not until the early 20th century that niobium was used in <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_lamp" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent lamp">incandescent lamp</a> filaments, the first commercial application.<sup id="cite_ref-Gupta_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This use quickly became obsolete through the replacement of niobium with <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, which has a higher melting point. That niobium improves the <a href="/wiki/Microalloyed_steel" title="Microalloyed steel">strength of steel</a> was first discovered in the 1920s, and this application remains its predominant use.<sup id="cite_ref-Gupta_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In 1961, the American physicist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eugene_Kunzler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eugene Kunzler (page does not exist)">Eugene Kunzler</a> and coworkers at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/Niobium-tin" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium-tin">niobium-tin</a> continues to exhibit superconductivity in the presence of strong electric currents and magnetic fields,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> making it the first material to support the high currents and fields necessary for useful high-power magnets and electrical power <a href="/wiki/Machinery" class="mw-redirect" title="Machinery">machinery</a>. This discovery enabled — two decades later — the production of long multi-strand cables wound into coils to create large, powerful <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnets</a> for rotating machinery, particle accelerators, and particle detectors.<sup id="cite_ref-geballe_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geballe-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_the_element">Naming the element</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Naming the element">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Columbium</i> (symbol "Cb")<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> was the name originally bestowed by Hatchett upon his discovery of the metal in 1801.<sup id="cite_ref-Hatchett_1802b_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatchett_1802b-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The name reflected that the type specimen of the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> came from America (<a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholson_1809_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson_1809-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> This name remained in use in American journals—the last paper published by <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> with <i>columbium</i> in its title dates from 1953<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>—while <i>niobium</i> was used in Europe. To end this confusion, the name <i>niobium</i> was chosen for element 41 at the 15th Conference of the Union of Chemistry in Amsterdam in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-Contro_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contro-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> A year later this name was officially adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) after 100 years of controversy, despite the chronological precedence of the name <i>columbium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Contro_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contro-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  This was a compromise of sorts;<sup id="cite_ref-Contro_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contro-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> the IUPAC accepted <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> instead of wolfram in deference to North American usage; and <i>niobium</i> instead of <i>columbium</i> in deference to European usage. While many US chemical societies and government organizations typically use the official IUPAC name, some metallurgists and metal societies still use the original American name, "<i>columbium</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-patel_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patel-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gree_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gree-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Niobium is a <a href="/wiki/Lustre_(mineralogy)" title="Lustre (mineralogy)">lustrous</a>, grey, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_5_element" title="Group 5 element">group 5</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> (see table), with an electron configuration in the outermost <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">shells</a> atypical for group 5. (This can be observed in the neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> (44), <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">rhodium</a> (45), and <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> (46).)
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin:10px; float:right;">

<tbody><tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a></th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">Element</a></th>

</th></tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a></td>

</td></tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Dubnium" title="Dubnium">dubnium</a></td>
<td>2, 8, 18, 32, 32, 11, 2
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Although it is thought to have a <a href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a> crystal structure from absolute zero to its melting point, high-resolution measurements of the thermal expansion along the three crystallographic axes reveal anisotropies which are inconsistent with a cubic structure.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, further research and discovery in this area is expected.
</p><p>Niobium becomes a <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> temperatures. At atmospheric pressure, it has the highest critical temperature of the elemental superconductors at 9.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pein_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Niobium has the greatest <a href="/wiki/Superconductor#Meissner_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">magnetic penetration depth</a> of any element.<sup id="cite_ref-Pein_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In addition, it is one of the three elemental <a href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Type II superconductor">Type II superconductors</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>. The superconductive properties are strongly dependent on the purity of the niobium metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Moura_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moura-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When very pure, it is comparatively soft and ductile, but impurities make it harder.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The metal has a low <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture#Capture_cross_section" title="Neutron capture">capture cross-section</a> for thermal <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> thus it is used in the nuclear industries where neutron transparent structures are desired.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The metal takes on a bluish tinge when exposed to air at room temperature for extended periods.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubber_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubber-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Despite a high melting point in elemental form (2,468&#160;°C), it has a lower density than other refractory metals. Furthermore, it is corrosion-resistant, exhibits superconductivity properties, and forms <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> layers.
</p><p>Niobium is slightly less <a href="/wiki/Electropositive" class="mw-redirect" title="Electropositive">electropositive</a> and more compact than its predecessor in the periodic table, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, whereas it is virtually identical in size to the heavier tantalum atoms, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide contraction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> As a result, niobium's chemical properties are very similar to those for tantalum, which appears directly below niobium in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gupta_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Although its corrosion resistance is not as outstanding as that of tantalum, the lower price and greater availability make niobium attractive for less demanding applications, such as vat linings in chemical plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_niobium" title="Isotopes of niobium">Isotopes of niobium</a></div>
<p>Niobium in the Earth's crust comprises one stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, <sup>93</sup>Nb.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> By 2003, at least 32 <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> had been synthesized, ranging in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> from 81 to 113. The most stable of these is <sup>92</sup>Nb with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 34.7&#160;million years. One of the least stable is <sup>113</sup>Nb, with an estimated half-life of 30&#160;milliseconds. Isotopes that are lighter than the stable <sup>93</sup>Nb tend to decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>+</sup> decay</a>, and those that are heavier tend to decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup> decay</a>, with some exceptions. <sup>81</sup>Nb, <sup>82</sup>Nb, and <sup>84</sup>Nb have minor β<sup>+</sup> delayed <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a> decay paths, <sup>91</sup>Nb decays by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a>, and <sup>92</sup>Nb decays by both <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">β<sup>+</sup></a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">β<sup>−</sup></a> decay.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At least 25 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a> have been described, ranging in atomic mass from 84 to 104. Within this range, only <sup>96</sup>Nb, <sup>101</sup>Nb, and <sup>103</sup>Nb do not have isomers. The most stable of niobium's isomers is <sup>93m</sup>Nb with a half-life of 16.13&#160;years. The least stable isomer is <sup>84m</sup>Nb with a half-life of 103&#160;ns. All of niobium's isomers decay by <a href="/wiki/Isomeric_transition" class="mw-redirect" title="Isomeric transition">isomeric transition</a> or beta decay except <sup>92m1</sup>Nb, which has a minor electron capture branch.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Niobium_minerals" title="Category:Niobium minerals">Category:Niobium minerals</a></div>
<p>Niobium is estimated to be the 34th <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">most common element in the Earth’s crust</a>, with 20&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Some think that the abundance on Earth is much greater, and that the element's high density has concentrated it in the Earth’s core.<sup id="cite_ref-patel_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patel-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The free element is not found in nature, but niobium occurs in combination with other elements in minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Minerals that contain niobium often also contain tantalum.  Examples include <a href="/wiki/Ferrocolumbite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrocolumbite">columbite</a> ((Fe,Mn)(Nb,Ta)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Coltan" title="Coltan">columbite–tantalite</a> (or <i>coltan</i>, (Fe,Mn)(Ta,Nb)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-ICE_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICE-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Columbite–tantalite minerals (the most common species being columbite-(Fe) and tantalite-(Fe), where "-(Fe)" is the Levinson suffix informing about the prevailence of iron over other elements like manganese<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>) are most usually found as accessory minerals in <a href="/wiki/Pegmatite" title="Pegmatite">pegmatite</a> intrusions, and in alkaline intrusive rocks. Less common are the niobates of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a>.  Examples of such niobates are <a href="/wiki/Pyrochlore" title="Pyrochlore">pyrochlore</a> ((Na,Ca)<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>(OH,F)) (now a group name, with a relatively common example being, e.g., fluorcalciopyrochlore<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Euxenite" title="Euxenite">euxenite</a> (correctly named euxenite-(Y)<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>) ((Y,Ca,Ce,U,Th)(Nb,Ta,Ti)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>). These large deposits of niobium have been found associated with <a href="/wiki/Carbonatite" title="Carbonatite">carbonatites</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carbonate_minerals" title="Carbonate minerals">carbonate</a>-<a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rocks" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous rocks">igneous rocks</a>) and as a constituent of pyrochlore.<sup id="cite_ref-Pyrochlore_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pyrochlore-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The three largest currently mined deposits of pyrochlore, two in Brazil and one in Canada, were found in the 1950s, and are still the major producers of niobium mineral concentrates.<sup id="cite_ref-Gupta_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The largest deposit is hosted within a carbonatite intrusion in <a href="/wiki/Arax%C3%A1" title="Araxá">Araxá</a>, state of <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">Minas Gerais</a>, Brazil, owned by CBMM (<a href="/wiki/Companhia_Brasileira_de_Metalurgia_e_Minera%C3%A7%C3%A3o" title="Companhia Brasileira de Metalurgia e Mineração">Companhia Brasileira de Metalurgia e Mineração</a>); the other active Brazilian deposit is located near <a href="/wiki/Catal%C3%A3o" title="Catalão">Catalão</a>, state of <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">Goiás</a>, and owned by <a href="/wiki/China_Molybdenum" title="China Molybdenum">China Molybdenum</a>, also hosted within a carbonatite intrusion.<sup id="cite_ref-tesla_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tesla-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Together, those two mines produce about 88% of the world's supply.<sup id="cite_ref-g1_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g1-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Brazil also has a large but still unexploited deposit near <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Gabriel_da_Cachoeira" title="São Gabriel da Cachoeira">São Gabriel da Cachoeira</a>, state of <a href="/wiki/Amazonas_(Brazilian_state)" title="Amazonas (Brazilian state)">Amazonas</a>, as well as a few smaller deposits, notably in the state of <a href="/wiki/Roraima" title="Roraima">Roraima</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-g1_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g1-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The third largest producer of niobium is the carbonatite-hosted <a href="/wiki/Niobec" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobec">Niobec</a> mine, in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Honor%C3%A9,_Quebec" title="Saint-Honoré, Quebec">Saint-Honoré</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chicoutimi" title="Chicoutimi">Chicoutimi</a>, Quebec, Canada, owned by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magris_Resources&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Magris Resources (page does not exist)">Magris Resources</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-niobec-magris_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niobec-magris-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> It produces between 7% and 10% of the world's supply.<sup id="cite_ref-tesla_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tesla-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-g1_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g1-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg" class="image"><img alt="Grey and white world map with Brazil colored red representing 90% of niobium world production and Canada colored in dark blue representing 5% of niobium world production" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg/310px-World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg.png" width="310" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg/465px-World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg/620px-World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="415" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Niobium_Production_2006.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Niobium producers in 2006 to 2015</div></div></div>
<p>After the separation from the other minerals, the mixed oxides of tantalum <a href="/wiki/Tantalum_pentoxide" title="Tantalum pentoxide">Ta<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub></a> and niobium <a href="/wiki/Niobium_pentoxide" title="Niobium pentoxide">Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub></a> are obtained. The first step in the processing is the reaction of the oxides with <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-ICE_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICE-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Ta<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> + 14 HF → 2 H<sub>2</sub>[TaF<sub>7</sub>] + 5 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> + 10 HF → 2 H<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>] + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The first industrial scale separation, developed by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Charles_Galissard_de_Marignac" title="Jean Charles Galissard de Marignac">de Marignac</a>, exploits the differing solubilities of the complex niobium and tantalum <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluorides</a>, dipotassium oxypentafluoroniobate monohydrate (K<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>]·H<sub>2</sub>O) and dipotassium heptafluorotantalate (K<sub>2</sub>[TaF<sub>7</sub>]) in water. Newer processes use the liquid extraction of the fluorides from <a href="/wiki/Aqueous" class="mw-redirect" title="Aqueous">aqueous</a> solution by <a href="/wiki/Organic_solvents" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic solvents">organic solvents</a> like <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexanone" title="Cyclohexanone">cyclohexanone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ICE_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICE-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The complex niobium and tantalum fluorides are extracted separately from the <a href="/wiki/Organic_solvent" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic solvent">organic solvent</a> with water and either precipitated by the addition of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">potassium fluoride</a> to produce a potassium fluoride complex, or precipitated with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> as the pentoxide:<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>] + 2 KF → K<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>]↓ + 2 HF</dd></dl>
<p>Followed by:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 H<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>] + 10 NH<sub>4</sub>OH → Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>↓ + 10 NH<sub>4</sub>F + 7 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Several methods are used for the <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reduction (chemistry)">reduction</a> to metallic niobium. The <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of a molten mixture of K<sub>2</sub>[NbOF<sub>5</sub>] and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> is one; the other is the reduction of the fluoride with <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>. With this method, a relatively high purity niobium can be obtained. In large scale production, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is reduced with hydrogen or carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/Aluminothermic_reaction" title="Aluminothermic reaction">aluminothermic reaction</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> and niobium oxide is reacted with <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>3 Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 12 Al → 6 Nb + 2 Fe + 6 Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Small amounts of oxidizers like <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> are added to enhance the reaction. The result is <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferroniobium" title="Ferroniobium">ferroniobium</a>, an alloy of iron and niobium used in the steel production.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Ferroniobium contains between 60 and 70% niobium.<sup id="cite_ref-tesla_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tesla-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Without iron oxide, the aluminothermic process is used to produce niobium. Further purification is necessary to reach the grade for <a href="/wiki/Superconductive" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductive">superconductive</a> alloys. <a href="/wiki/Electron_beam_melting" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron beam melting">Electron beam melting</a> under vacuum is the method used by the two major distributors of niobium.<sup id="cite_ref-Aguly_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aguly-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chou_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chou-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Companhia_Brasileira_de_Metalurgia_e_Minera%C3%A7%C3%A3o" title="Companhia Brasileira de Metalurgia e Mineração">CBMM</a> from Brazil controlled 85 percent of the world's niobium production.<sup id="cite_ref-lucchesi2013_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lucchesi2013-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> estimates that the production increased from 38,700 tonnes in 2005 to 44,500 tonnes in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2006_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2006-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2007_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2007-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>  Worldwide resources are estimated to be 4,400,000 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2007_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2007-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> During the ten-year period between 1995 and 2005, the production more than doubled, starting from 17,800 tonnes in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS1997_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS1997-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Between 2009 and 2011, production was stable at  63,000 tonnes per year,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> with a slight decrease in 2012 to only 50,000 tonnes per year.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
