</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Niobium_compounds" title="Category:Niobium compounds">Category:Niobium compounds</a></div>
<p>In many ways, niobium is similar to <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>. It reacts with most nonmetals at high temperatures; with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> at room temperature; with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> at 200&#160;°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a>; and with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> at 400&#160;°C, with products that are frequently interstitial and nonstoichiometric.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The metal begins to <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidize</a> in air at 200&#160;°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> It resists corrosion by fused <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkalis</a> and by acids, including <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Niobium is attacked by <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> and hydrofluoric/nitric acid mixtures.
</p><p>Although niobium exhibits all of the formal oxidation states from +5 to −1, the most common compounds have niobium in the +5 state.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Characteristically, compounds in oxidation states less than 5+ display Nb–Nb bonding.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_sulfides">Oxides and sulfides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Oxides and sulfides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Niobium forms <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> +5 (<a href="/wiki/Niobium_pentoxide" title="Niobium pentoxide">Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub></a>),<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> +4 (<a href="/wiki/Niobium_dioxide" title="Niobium dioxide">NbO<sub>2</sub></a>), +3 (<span class="chemf nowrap">Nb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and the rarer oxidation state, +2 (<a href="/wiki/Niobium_monoxide" title="Niobium monoxide">NbO</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Most common is the pentoxide, precursor to almost all niobium compounds and alloys.<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cardarelli_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cardarelli-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Niobates are generated by dissolving the pentoxide in <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> solutions or by melting it in alkali metal oxides. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Lithium_niobate" title="Lithium niobate">lithium niobate</a> (LiNbO<sub>3</sub>) and lanthanum niobate (LaNbO<sub>4</sub>). In the lithium niobate is a trigonally distorted <a href="/wiki/Perovskite_(structure)" title="Perovskite (structure)">perovskite</a>-like structure, whereas the lanthanum niobate contains lone <span class="chemf nowrap">NbO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ions.<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The layered niobium sulfide (NbS<sub>2</sub>) is also known.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_31-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Materials can be coated with a thin film of niobium(V) oxide <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">chemical vapor deposition</a> or <a href="/wiki/Atomic_layer_deposition" title="Atomic layer deposition">atomic layer deposition</a> processes, produced by the thermal decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Niobium(V)_ethoxide" title="Niobium(V) ethoxide">niobium(V) ethoxide</a> above 350&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Watch glass on a black surface with a small portion of yellow crystals" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg/180px-Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg/270px-Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg/360px-Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium_pentachloride_solid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample of niobium pentachloride (yellow portion) that has partially hydrolyzed (white material).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/180px-Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" width="180" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/270px-Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/360px-Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium-pentachloride-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ball-and-stick model of <a href="/wiki/Niobium_pentachloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium pentachloride">niobium pentachloride</a>, which exists as a <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a></div></div></div>
<p>Niobium forms halides in the oxidation states of +5 and +4 as well as diverse <a href="/wiki/Nonstoichiometric_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonstoichiometric compound">substoichiometric compounds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_54-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aguly_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aguly-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The pentahalides (<span class="chemf nowrap">NbX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>) feature octahedral Nb centres. Niobium pentafluoride (NbF<sub>5</sub>) is a white solid with a melting point of 79.0&#160;°C and <a href="/wiki/Niobium_pentachloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium pentachloride">niobium pentachloride</a> (NbCl<sub>5</sub>) is yellow (see image at left) with a melting point of 203.4&#160;°C. Both are <a href="/wiki/Hydrolyzed" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrolyzed">hydrolyzed</a> to give oxides and oxyhalides, such as NbOCl<sub>3</sub>. The pentachloride is a versatile reagent used to generate the <a href="/wiki/Organometallic" class="mw-redirect" title="Organometallic">organometallic</a> compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Niobocene_dichloride" title="Niobocene dichloride">niobocene dichloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>NbCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The tetrahalides (<span class="chemf nowrap">NbX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) are dark-coloured polymers with Nb-Nb bonds; for example, the black <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> niobium tetrafluoride (NbF<sub>4</sub>) and brown niobium tetrachloride (NbCl<sub>4</sub>).
</p><p>Anionic halide compounds of niobium are well known, owing in part to the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acidity</a> of the pentahalides. The most important is [NbF<sub>7</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>, an intermediate in the separation of Nb and Ta from the ores.<sup id="cite_ref-ICE_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICE-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This heptafluoride tends to form the oxopentafluoride more readily than does the tantalum compound. Other halide complexes include octahedral [NbCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup>:
</p>
<dl><dd>Nb<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>10</sub> + 2 Cl<sup>−</sup> → 2 [NbCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup></dd></dl>
<p>As with other metals with low atomic numbers, a variety of reduced halide cluster ions is known, the prime example being [Nb<sub>6</sub>Cl<sub>18</sub>]<sup>4−</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrides_and_carbides">Nitrides and carbides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nitrides and carbides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Binary_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary compound">binary compounds</a> of niobium include <a href="/wiki/Niobium_nitride" title="Niobium nitride">niobium nitride</a> (NbN), which becomes a <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> at low temperatures and is used in detectors for infrared light.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The main <a href="/wiki/Niobium_carbide" title="Niobium carbide">niobium carbide</a> is NbC, an extremely <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Refraction_(metallurgy)" title="Refraction (metallurgy)">refractory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> material, commercially used in cutting <a href="/wiki/Tool_bit" title="Tool bit">tool bits</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium_metal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three pieces of metallic foil with yellow taint" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Niobium_metal.jpg/220px-Niobium_metal.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Niobium_metal.jpg/330px-Niobium_metal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Niobium_metal.jpg/440px-Niobium_metal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="1209" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niobium_metal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A niobium foil</div></div></div>
<p>Out of 44,500 tonnes of niobium mined in 2006, an estimated 90% was used in high-grade structural steel. The second largest application is <a href="/wiki/Superalloys" class="mw-redirect" title="Superalloys">superalloys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2006_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2006-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Niobium alloy superconductors and electronic components account for a very small share of the world production.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS2006_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS2006-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steel_production">Steel production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Steel production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Niobium is an effective <a href="/wiki/Microalloyed_steel" title="Microalloyed steel">microalloying</a> element for steel, within which it forms <a href="/wiki/Niobium_carbide" title="Niobium carbide">niobium carbide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niobium_nitride" title="Niobium nitride">niobium nitride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-patel_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patel-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> These compounds improve the grain refining, and retard recrystallization and precipitation hardening. These effects in turn increase the toughness, strength, formability, and weldability.<sup id="cite_ref-patel_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patel-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Within microalloyed <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steels</a>, the niobium content is a small (less than 0.1%<sup id="cite_ref-heister_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heister-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>) but important addition to <a href="/wiki/HSLA_steel" class="mw-redirect" title="HSLA steel">high strength low alloy steels</a> that are widely used structurally in modern automobiles.<sup id="cite_ref-patel_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patel-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Niobium is sometimes used in considerably higher quantities for highly wear-resistant machine components and knives, as high as 3% in Crucible CPM S110V stainless steel.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These same niobium alloys are often used in pipeline construction.<sup id="cite_ref-eggert_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eggert-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hillenbrand_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hillenbrand-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superalloys">Superalloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Superalloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Image of the Apollo Service Module with the moon in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/220px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/330px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/440px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="904" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 15 CSM in lunar orbit with <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Service_Propulsion_System" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">the dark rocket nozzle</a> made from <a href="/wiki/Niobium-titanium" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium-titanium">niobium-titanium</a> alloy</div></div></div>
<p>Quantities of niobium are used in nickel-, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>-, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">superalloys</a> in proportions as great as 6.5%<sup id="cite_ref-heister_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heister-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> for such applications as <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engine</a> components, <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbines</a>, rocket subassemblies, turbo charger systems, heat resisting, and combustion equipment. Niobium precipitates a hardening γ''-phase within the grain structure of the superalloy.<sup id="cite_ref-Donachie_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donachie-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One example superalloy is <a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">Inconel 718</a>, consisting of roughly 50% <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, 18.6% <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, 18.5% <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, 5% niobium, 3.1% <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, 0.9% <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, and 0.4% <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-super_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-super-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> These superalloys were used, for example, in advanced air frame systems for the <a href="/wiki/Gemini_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Gemini program">Gemini program</a>. Another niobium alloy was used for the nozzle of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_service_module" class="mw-redirect" title="Apollo service module">Apollo Service Module</a>. Because niobium is oxidized at temperatures above 400&#160;°C, a protective coating is necessary for these applications to prevent the alloy from becoming brittle.<sup id="cite_ref-hightemp_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hightemp-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niobium-based_alloys">Niobium-based alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Niobium-based alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>C-103</b> alloy was developed in the early 1960s jointly by the <a href="/wiki/Wah_Chang_Corporation" title="Wah Chang Corporation">Wah Chang Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> Co.  <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Union_Carbide" title="Union Carbide">Union Carbide</a> Corp., <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> Co. and several other companies were developing <a href="/wiki/Niobium_alloy" title="Niobium alloy">Nb-base alloys</a> simultaneously, largely driven by the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>.  It is composed of 89% niobium, 10% <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a> and 1% titanium and is used for <a href="/wiki/Liquid_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid rocket">liquid rocket</a> thruster nozzles, such as the main engine of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Apollo Lunar Modules</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hightemp_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hightemp-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The nozzle of the <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(rocket_engine_family)#Merlin_Vacuum_(1C)" title="Merlin (rocket engine family)">Merlin Vacuum</a> series of engines developed by <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> for the upper stage of its <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9" title="Falcon 9">Falcon 9</a> rocket is made from a niobium alloy.<sup id="cite_ref-NSPO_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSPO-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The reactivity of niobium with oxygen requires it to be worked in a <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">vacuum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert atmosphere</a>, which significantly increases the cost and difficulty of production.  <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_arc_remelting" title="Vacuum arc remelting">Vacuum arc remelting</a> (VAR) and <a href="/wiki/Electron_beam_melting" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron beam melting">electron beam melting</a> (EBM), novel processes at the time, enabled the development of niobium and other reactive metals.  The project that yielded C-103 began in 1959 with as many as 256 experimental niobium alloys in the "C-series" (possibly from <b>c</b>olumbium) that could be melted as buttons and rolled into <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">sheet</a>.  Wah Chang had an inventory of hafnium, refined from nuclear-grade <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_alloy" title="Zirconium alloy">zirconium alloys</a>, that it wanted to put to commercial use.  The 103rd experimental composition of the C-series alloys, Nb-10Hf-1Ti, had the best combination of formability and high-temperature properties. Wah Chang fabricated the first 500-lb heat of C-103 in 1961, ingot to sheet, using EBM and VAR. The intended applications included <a href="/wiki/Turbine_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Turbine engine">turbine engines</a> and liquid metal <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchangers</a>.  Competing niobium alloys from that era included FS85 (Nb-10W-28Ta-1Zr) from Fansteel Metallurgical Corp., Cb129Y (Nb-10W-10Hf-0.2Y) from Wah Chang and Boeing, Cb752 (Nb-10W-2.5Zr) from Union Carbide, and Nb1Zr from Superior Tube Co.<sup id="cite_ref-hightemp_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hightemp-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superconducting_magnets">Superconducting magnets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Superconducting magnets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Modern_3T_MRI.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Room-high yellow-grey medical machine with a man-size hole in the middle and a stretcher directly in front of it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/220px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/330px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/440px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Modern_3T_MRI.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 3-<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">tesla</a> clinical <a href="/wiki/MRI" class="mw-redirect" title="MRI">magnetic resonance imaging</a> scanner using niobium superconducting alloy</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Niobium-germanium" title="Niobium-germanium">Niobium-germanium</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Nb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Ge</span>), <a href="/wiki/Niobium-tin" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium-tin">niobium-tin</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Nb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Sn</span>), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Niobium-titanium" class="mw-redirect" title="Niobium-titanium">niobium-titanium</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> are used as a <a href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Type II superconductor">type II superconductor</a> wire for <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">superconducting magnets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> These superconducting magnets are used in <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> instruments as well as in <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> uses 600 tons of superconducting strands, while the <a href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor</a> uses an estimated 600 tonnes of Nb<sub>3</sub>Sn strands and 250 tonnes of NbTi strands.<sup id="cite_ref-alstrom_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alstrom-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 1992 alone, more than US$1&#160;billion worth of clinical magnetic resonance imaging systems were constructed with niobium-titanium wire.<sup id="cite_ref-geballe_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geballe-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_superconductors">Other superconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other superconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG/220px-A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG/330px-A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG/440px-A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_1.3_GHz_nine-cell_superconducting_radio_frequency.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1.3&#160;GHz SRF made from niobium is on display at <a href="/wiki/Fermilab" title="Fermilab">Fermilab</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_radio_frequency" title="Superconducting radio frequency">superconducting radio frequency</a> (SRF) cavities used in the <a href="/wiki/Free-electron_laser" title="Free-electron laser">free-electron lasers</a> <a href="/wiki/FLASH" title="FLASH">FLASH</a> (result of the cancelled TESLA linear accelerator project) and <a href="/wiki/European_x-ray_free_electron_laser" class="mw-redirect" title="European x-ray free electron laser">XFEL</a> are made from pure niobium.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Cryomodule" title="Cryomodule">cryomodule</a> team at <a href="/wiki/Fermilab" title="Fermilab">Fermilab</a> used the same SRF technology from the FLASH project to develop 1.3&#160;GHz nine-cell SRF cavities made from pure niobium. The cavities will be used in the 30-kilometre (19&#160;mi) <a href="/wiki/Linear_particle_accelerator" title="Linear particle accelerator">linear particle accelerator</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Linear_Collider" title="International Linear Collider">International Linear Collider</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The same technology will be used in <a href="/wiki/LCLS-II" class="mw-redirect" title="LCLS-II">LCLS-II</a> at <a href="/wiki/SLAC_National_Accelerator_Laboratory" title="SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory">SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/PIP-II" class="mw-redirect" title="PIP-II">PIP-II</a> at Fermilab.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The high sensitivity of superconducting niobium nitride <a href="/wiki/Bolometer" title="Bolometer">bolometers</a> make them an ideal detector for <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> in the THz frequency band. These detectors were tested at the <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz_Submillimeter_Telescope" title="Heinrich Hertz Submillimeter Telescope">Submillimeter Telescope</a>, the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole_Telescope" title="South Pole Telescope">South Pole Telescope</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Receiver_Lab_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Receiver Lab Telescope (page does not exist)">Receiver Lab Telescope</a>, and at <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Pathfinder_Experiment" title="Atacama Pathfinder Experiment">APEX</a>, and are now used in the HIFI instrument on board the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electroceramics">Electroceramics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Electroceramics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_niobate" title="Lithium niobate">Lithium niobate</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Ferroelectric" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferroelectric">ferroelectric</a>, is used extensively in mobile telephones and <a href="/wiki/Optical_modulators" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical modulators">optical modulators</a>, and for the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Surface_acoustic_wave" title="Surface acoustic wave">surface acoustic wave</a> devices. It belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">ABO<sub>3</sub></a> structure ferroelectrics like <a href="/wiki/Lithium_tantalate" title="Lithium tantalate">lithium tantalate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barium_titanate" title="Barium titanate">barium titanate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niobium_capacitor" title="Niobium capacitor">Niobium capacitors</a> are available as alternative to  <a href="/wiki/Tantalum_capacitor" title="Tantalum capacitor">tantalum capacitors</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> but tantalum capacitors still predominate. Niobium is added to glass to obtain a higher <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>, making possible thinner and lighter <a href="/wiki/Glasses" title="Glasses">corrective glasses</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypoallergenic_applications:_medicine_and_jewelry">Hypoallergenic applications: medicine and jewelry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hypoallergenic applications: medicine and jewelry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Niobium and some niobium alloys are physiologically inert and <a href="/wiki/Hypoallergenic" title="Hypoallergenic">hypoallergenic</a>. For this reason, niobium is used in prosthetics and implant devices, such as pacemakers.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Niobium treated with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> forms a porous layer that aids <a href="/wiki/Osseointegration" title="Osseointegration">osseointegration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like titanium, tantalum, and aluminium, niobium can be heated and <a href="/wiki/Anodize" class="mw-redirect" title="Anodize">anodized</a> ("reactive metal <a href="/wiki/Anodizing" title="Anodizing">anodization</a>") to produce a wide array of <a href="/wiki/Iridescence" title="Iridescence">iridescent</a> colours for jewelry,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> where its hypoallergenic property is highly desirable.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numismatics">Numismatics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Numismatics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2004_Austria_25_Euro_150_Years_Semmering_Alpine_Railway_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coin with a dark green cener and a silvery outer rim. The rim reads: Republik Österreich 25 Euro. The centere shows electric and a steam driven locomotive" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c9/2004_Austria_25_Euro_150_Years_Semmering_Alpine_Railway_front.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="220" data-file-height="220" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2004_Austria_25_Euro_150_Years_Semmering_Alpine_Railway_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 150 Years <a href="/wiki/Semmering_railway" title="Semmering railway">Semmering Alpine Railway</a> Coin made of niobium and silver</div></div></div>
<p>Niobium is used as a precious metal in commemorative coins, often with silver or gold. For example, Austria produced a series of silver niobium <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> coins starting in 2003; the colour in these coins is created by the <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> of light by a thin anodized oxide layer.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, ten coins are available showing a broad variety of colours in the centre of the coin: blue, green, brown, purple, violet, or yellow. Two more examples are the 2004 Austrian €25 <a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Austria)#2004_coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Austria)">150 Years Semmering Alpine Railway commemorative coin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and the 2006 Austrian €25 <a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Austria)#2006_coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Austria)">European Satellite Navigation commemorative coin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
The Austrian mint produced for Latvia a similar series of coins starting in 2004,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
with one following in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
In 2011, the Royal Canadian Mint started production of a $5 <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a> and niobium coin named <i>Hunter's Moon</i><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
in which the niobium was selectively oxidized, thus creating unique finishes where no two coins are exactly alike.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The arc-tube seals of high pressure <a href="/wiki/Sodium_vapor_lamp" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium vapor lamp">sodium vapor lamps</a> are made from niobium, sometimes alloyed with 1% of <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>; niobium has a very similar coefficient of thermal expansion, matching the <a href="/wiki/Sintered" class="mw-redirect" title="Sintered">sintered</a> <a href="/wiki/Alumina" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumina">alumina</a> <a href="/wiki/Arc_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Arc tube">arc tube</a> ceramic, a translucent material which resists chemical attack or <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> by the hot liquid sodium and sodium vapour contained inside the operating lamp.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niobium is used in <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> rods for some stabilized grades of stainless steel<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and in anodes for cathodic protection systems on some water tanks, which are then usually plated with platinum.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niobium is an important component of high-performance heterogeneous catalysts for the production of acrylic acid by selective oxidation of propane.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niobium is used to make the high voltage wire of the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">Solar Corona</a> particles receptor module of the <a href="/wiki/Parker_Solar_Probe" title="Parker Solar Probe">Parker Solar Probe</a> <sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niobium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox bordered" style="border-collapse:collapse; width:22em; text-align:left;&#32;;">
<caption style="text-align:center;">Niobium
</caption>








<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background: #f8eaba; text-align: center;">Hazards
</th></tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Globally_Harmonized_System_of_Classification_and_Labelling_of_Chemicals" title="Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals">GHS signal word</a>
</td>
<td>Not listed as hazardous<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>







<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="container" style="margin:0 auto; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div id="on_image_elements" class="nounderlines" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_922892438"><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" coords="40,0,60,20,40,40,20,20" alt="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. <a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g., sodium chloride">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>Niobium has no known biological role. While niobium dust is an eye and skin irritant and a potential fire hazard, elemental niobium on a larger scale is physiologically inert (and thus hypoallergenic) and harmless. It is frequently used in jewelry and has been tested for use in some medical implants.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niobium-containing compounds are rarely encountered by most people, but some are toxic and should be treated with care. The short- and long-term exposure to niobates and niobium chloride, two chemicals that are water-soluble, have been tested in rats. Rats treated with a single injection of niobium pentachloride or niobates show a <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">median lethal dose</a> (LD<sub>50</sub>) between 10 and 100&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-Haley_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haley-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> For oral administration the toxicity is lower; a study with rats yielded a LD<sub>50</sub> after seven days of 940&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-Haley_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haley-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
